B000O8OTNC|5.0|This works great as a pocket sharpener to use on a knife just before and after use.  The swing out diamond stick is even useful to throw a touch up on a chainsaw chain if you know how to do it.  For what it is, it is the best one of its kind and I have tried as many as I have been able to find and buy.  It is the perfect thing for a tool box or tackle box for tuning up an edge as well as for backpacking so you can keep a blade sharp as you camp.  A knife's edge, if looked at under magnification has a number of small \\"feathers\\" along it.  These feathers are what make the edge really cut things easily, like really small serrations.  As an edge is used the feather become folded over and misaligned and what this sort of sharpener does well is straighten those back out so they can get back to work.<br /><br />What this will not do is sharpen a knife once it has become truly dull.  When the feathers eventually have been bent back and forth so  many times the fall/break off, the knife has become truly dull and now you need to really sharpen it.  This sharpener may be able to handle this but in the time it would take to do it you would be much better served to learn to use a stone or buy something like a Sharpmaker type sharpener to do this rather than try and do it with one of this sort of sharpener.  They really are meant just to keep thing going between real sharpenings and not for actually sharpening a dull knife.<br /><br />Since it does what it is intended to do and does it really well, I give it all five stars.

Then he fails to detail critical points like A/B testing, which may not be familiar to the intended audience of this title. B005HV6Y5W|4.0|MOVIE RATING:  4 stars (B+)<br /><br />One of the most mysterious and interesting auteur filmmakers out there right now is director Terrence Malick. No one else seems to inspire as many rumors, stories and myths about what they are up to as Malick. When will we see a new film from him? What will the film be about? What's the title of the film? Malick's movies tend to languish in the news for years simply titled as \\"Untitled Terrence Malick Film\\". But 2011 saw the birth, both literally and figuratively, of a brand new Malick movie: The Tree Of Life (2011). This is only Malick's fifth film in a career spanning over 40 years. But his five films are iconic pieces of filmmaking art. I've seen them all and I highly recommend you see each and every one to really understand any one of them. They cling together with a web of subliminal meaning that has taken me years to contemplate. My favorite, hands down, is The Thin Red Line (1998), the most masterful look at war, and man for that matter, ever made. It's one of my Top 10 movies of all time. It took me years and repeated viewings to fully appreciate it.<br /><br />Like all of Malick's films, upon first viewing The Tree Of Life (in the theater a year ago), I was confronted with reconciling my very high expectations for the film with the actual film that Malick delivered. It's taken a year of simmering inside me to really figure out how I feel about it. And after viewing it in its entirety for a 2nd time on blu-ray recently, I feel ready to discuss the film and my thoughts and feelings about it. While The Tree Of Life still makes me question its meaning and intentions, I appreciate it a little more than during my first time. It's a movie that makes it hard for me to love because it goes way outside the box of normal narrative storytelling. I love strong dialogue driven movies. And The Tree Of Life is not that kind of movie. In fact, this movie probably has less dialogue than any other feature length movie I love. But the subtext in the visuals, music, and feel of the film really got into me during this second viewing because my expectations of wanting dialogue and narrative were gone. Instead of constantly waiting for the movie to give me what I want, like I did the first time, I accepted it for what it is and got something else. Looking back on all 5 of Malick's movies now, I can see that they all do the same thing. None of them delivered what I wanted or expected from them the first time I saw them. But after letting that go, I was able to appreciate them on subsequent viewings. My initial rating for The Tree Of Life was 3.5 stars but I've since updated it to 4 stars.<br /><br />I'm not going to try to provide a narrative summary or character descriptions for this film since it really means nothing to understanding this film. Brad Pitt, Jessica Chastain, Sean Penn and the three young boys (Hunter McCracken, Laramie Eppler, and Tye Sheridan) who play Pitt and Chastain's sons are all fantastic in their performances. They are not showy performances with scene-chewing speeches, but they are powerfully quiet and subtle performances rooted in deep character development. It's so clear that they all lived in the shoes of their characters during production on this movie. Malick's style of shooting demands that anyways from what I understand. I think Malick really nailed the casting on this film. Pitt and Chastain couldn't be more different yet complimentary in their roles as universal representations of \\"nature\\" and \\"grace\\". They just work together in this story. I actually think the 3 boys did the most with their roles. Their very naturalistic performances really brought my own childhood memories and feelings to life through their eyes.<br /><br />As with every Malick film, the production design, cinematography, sound, music, and editing are simply the best. I don't know what else to say. There's no room for improvement in any of those areas and the craftsmanship on this movie is really beyond perfection, even though I know that's not possible. But that's the experience I get. It really is astounding to see Malick and his incredible team provide such a meticulous and beautiful film. It has a natural and organic feel to it, yet I know that every single frame of the film and second of sound has been masterfully thought about by someone. Somehow Malick's style of filmmaking follows all the rules and breaks all the rules at the same time. When it comes to pure technical filmmaking craft, I give The Tree Of Life 5 stars out of 5. It's only the story and the writing (assuming there was much), that knocks this film down a start for me. But it wouldn't surprise me that if the \\"secrets\\" of this movie are revealed to me down the road, that I upgrade my score.<br /><br />While I could go on and on talking about the details of this movie, I think it's best to let it speak for itself beyond what I've had to say so far. The ideas, lessons, questions, and queries that I think it poses can't really be talked about. I believe one has to internalize them like all philosophy, psychology, and spirituality. They're are no real answers in my mind from The Tree Of Life to the questions it asks. It's the questions that are the meat of it's meal.nd perfection, even though I know that's not possible. B004RFYEEC|5.0|Although I've owned this big box set since it first came out, I've taken my time reacting to it.  And time, it seems, has confirmed the crucial-- no, cosmic-- contribution of the Beach Boys to the music of the past half-century.  This set belongs in archival collections everywhere for the study (and ultimate exasperation) of musical scholars everywhere.  SMiLE fanatics who have collected or compiled bootlegs over the past 20 or more years will probably want the big box, too:  even for them, there are some nice surprises.  For Beach Boys fans otherwise, or for those not prepared to pay such a steep price, the 2-CD edition should more than suffice.  My comments apply generally to both sets.<br /><br />1.  This is not the SMiLE album.  As many other reviewers have pointed out, SMiLE was a Beach Boys project undertaken from late 1966 through mid-1967 that ended as a train wreck whose non-appearance derailed their careers for years afterward and permanently curtailed Brian Wilson's lofty ambitions.  What we have on Disc 1, tracks 1-19 is essentially the stage show cleverly put together by Brian and his cohorts for a series of performances and documented on the 2004 CD \\"Brian Wilson Presents SMiLE.\\"  The knock against that one, fine as it was, is that it wasn't the Beach Boys (a legitimate knock, IMO).  The knock against this one is that it remains a stage show, a collection of songs arranged into three movements.  The songs themselves are brilliant, often transcendent, but as an \\"album,\\" the whole is less than the sum of its parts, far from the modular production style Brian was pioneering in 1966.  Had he realized his ambition at that time, although we'll never know what the vinyl LP would have been, I feel sure it would have been something quite beyond a mere collection of songs.  As \\"Good Vibrations\\" is less a song than a \\"pocket symphony,\\" SMiLE would have challenged the idea of \\"song\\" by extending the concept to album length.  The session excerpts on the remaining discs often bear out the intended \\"wholeness\\" of SMiLE, where nearly any fragment could be reflected or repeated elsewhere in the album.<br /><br />2.  The track I return to most is on Disc 1, track 25, an 8+ minute compilation of Beach Boys backing vocals.  No other vocal group, then or now, could conceive, much less perform, these astonishing excerpts.  The track also serves to remind us that SMiLE, much more so than Pet Sounds, was a Beach Boys project, not a Brian Wilson confessional.<br /><br />3.  Another plus for the big box set.  However often repeated-- \\"Heroes and Villains,\\" parts 1 and 2, \\"Vegetables,\\"  and others, they are almost never duplicates of each other, so there are many unexpected shifts within songs.  Nice, too, to have the stereo versions on the final LP side, though, as other reviewers have complained, the vinyl pressings are not all that they could have been.<br /><br />4.  The \\"fly-ins\\"-- material borrowed from sessions other than the originals and synced-up to the original backing tracks-- I find rather bothersome on several levels.  For one thing, the producers are inconsistent:  while it makes sense to use Carl Wilson's lead vocal, recorded in 1968, on \\"Cabinessence,\\" why not include the 20/20 album's enhanced version of \\"Our Prayer,\\" at least as an appendix?  \\"Barnyard\\" and \\"I'm in Great Shape,\\" with their extraneous vocals, do not add much interest.  As for \\"Surf's Up,\\" with Brian's lead vocals properly in sync with the backing track and added enhancements from the album version:  it works, but the jury is still out as to whether I really like it.<br /><br />5.  Couldn't the producers have isolated Dennis Wilson's recitation--still all but inaudible-- behind the second chorus of \\"Cabinessence\\"?<br /><br />All cavils notwithstanding, it would be a travesty to rate this set with anything less than the highest recommendations.  SMiLE remains the pivotal point of the Beach Boys' career, and it remains a mystery yet on so many levels.  I hope this set remains in print forever.

193413712X|5.0|In his 1946 lecture Existentialism is a Humanism, Jean-Paul Sartre strikes a familiar existentialist chord:  the world is absurd in that it conforms to nobody's will and cannot be relied upon to further even the most sincere and fervent of aims. Sartre writes:<br /><br />For there is no God and no prevenient design,which can adapt the world and all its possibilities to my will. When Descartes said, \\"Conquer yourself rather than the world,\\" what he meant was, at bottom, the same - that we should act without hope.<br /><br />To face the absurdity of the world is to live in what Sartre calls \\"despair.\\"  One seldom faces it, soldiering on as if one could make the world ones own, could order it according to the rational structures one has been taught to embrace, could weave it seamlessly into the auto-narrative that is ones life.  In Tinkers the heroic, futile, irrationally faithful efforts of the characters to create themselves as fathers, sons, mothers, wives are chronicled by Paul Harding.  With wisdom, gentleness, and love he reveals the deeply and intensely felt yearnings of characters beset by their own infirmities, the vicissitudes of the \\"riotous\\" natural world, and the failures of their flawed theories of mind.<br /><br />To be human is to be confronted with one's conscious will and the inescapable fact that one must create oneself.  One can attempt to obliterate the gap between oneself and nature, as when Howard attempts to lose himself, Zen-like, in a meadow or a pond; or one can accept the gap pretending, ala George, that the world can be drawn or constructed according to ones own designs, like a blueprint or a clock.  Or one can, at ones own peril, ignore the gap, living as Howard's father did, in a narrative that floats farther and farther above natura firma until it bursts like a soap bubble, into noiseless insubstance.  Tinkers is about living in the gap.  We are born to need each other, doomed by the nature of selves to live alone.  So the space in whichwe live is not just between the conscious will and nature, but between ourselves and other selves.  Kathleen cannot unite herself with her husband or her children: she constructs a rigid parental grid in which to keep her sons and daughters and a wordless, impenetrable wall behind which to banish her broken husband.  Howard mistakes his wife's silence for an unspoken agreement founded in mutual love; and Howard, unable to bear the pain his affliction visits on his son never brings himself to touch the boy with his love.<br /><br />For some, life in the gap is too much and they are left insane, or resentful, or spent.  Others manage to create and sustain mirages that are close enough to reality that they come apart only when their creators are too weak to sustain them any longer.  Then they come crashing down like the roof and floors of the dying George's house, leaving shards which can never be reassembled because their unity was, after all, the grandest part of the illusion.<br /><br />Tinkers is about what Harding calls \\"the deep and secret yes,\\" about the utter existential impossibility of being human juxtaposed against the brave, precious, faith that the impossible can transcend itself.<br /><br />An exquisite artist whose medium is words, Harding's specialty is pastoral landscapes and the portraits of the souls who occupy them.  He saturates the page with color (a master of silver hues and textures) and paints with light every bit as well as the most accomplished oil or acrylic artist.  Harding's novel is at once a celebration of the spirit and grace of the natural world and a penetrating psychological homage to the individual human being and his foibles.  Tinkers is a lovely and heart-wrenching thing of beauty.

B000E41MRC|5.0|In 1934, Adolph Hitler's National Socialist Party was in power in Germany, but not yet in total control of the country.  Hitler commissioned actress and director Leni Riefenstahl to film the 1934 Nazi Party Congress in Nuremberg, which was to be used as a propaganda film inside and outside of Germany, to entice more of the German population to support the Nazi party, or at least consider it as a viable alternative to other parties. The resulting \\"documentary\\" was called \\"Triumph of the Will\\".<br /><br />The film was legitimately very popular in Germany when released in 1935 ... purportedly one of the top three films of the year.  In a way, the film is similar to the Democratic and Republic national conventions held during the presidential election cycles; the production is carefully staged, with many speakers, a unifying theme, and attempts to convince the outside viewers of the \\"rightness\\" of its positions.  However the scale of the Nazi Party Congress was vast, with upwards of one million Germans attending, as opposed to the few thousands that might attend the national conventions, and the tone much, much more beligent.<br /><br />The film can be viewed in several ways.  First, as a documentary record of the 1934 Nazi Party Congress.  To today's audience, I would have to say it would be tedious and dull when viewed in this context.  Other than for the novelty of seeing and hearing Hitler and his cronies speak, the film wouldn't offer the casual viewer much in the way of entertainment.<br /><br />Second, as a historical microcosm of what was happening in Germany in 1934, from the Nazi party's stated agenda to viewing a small slice of German life from that time period.  The movie is more interesting when viewed that way.  If you're a World War II buff, or a historian of the time frame, all of the German bugaboos are on display, from racial purity to threatening to crush any enemies that stand in their way.  And the fervor of the German's attending the congress isn't being faked ... this was a meeting of the believers, and it showed.<br /><br />Finally, and most importantly, this film can be viewed as the technical masterpiece that it is.  Although it looks trite and repetitive by today's standards, many of the techniques used today were pioneered by Riefenstahl in this film ... moving cameras, low angle shot of important people/events, filming from an aircraft, and so on.  Although the film is not technically perfect ... you can see other cameras in the background and people will occasionally walk in front of a shot ... the fact that it was being filmed \\"live\\" shows what effort and preplanning went into the making of the film.  (Although Riefenstahl did re-film some of the speeches later and edit them back in with the \\"live\\" portions.)  This film is justifiably listed as one of the 100 best films on many lists primarily due to its lasting impact on how films were made.<br /><br />While the message being presented by the film is odious and repugnant, the film itself is both an important historical record and a technical masterpiece.  If you're a World War II buff, you need to watch this film.  Five stars.being faked ... this was a meeting of the believers, and it showed.<br /><br />Finally, and most importantly, this film can be viewed as the technical masterpiece that it is. B007L96SI6|5.0|As a huge fan of Haley Reinhart since the earliest days of her Season 10 American Idol run, Flask Man has eagerly, hungrily and obsessively anticipated the seductive siren's debut album since her elimination from the show during Top 3 week last May.<br /><br />At times, it seemed as if album day would never come.<br /><br />Beginning with her October move to LA, Haley almost immediately began logging countless hours in the studio, feverishly writing and recording album tracks with some of the music industry's top producers and songwriters. This intensive, 3 month creative period would result in her recording an album that by any measure should be considered an incredible debut effort.<br /><br />It also showed us something we didn't know: Haley is a damn good song writer. She technically may only be 21 years old, but the girl has been infused with music since birth by her parents, who have had their own rock band since before Haley was born. Her knowledge, appreciation and understanding of music history runs deep, and she certainly showed she has the ability to draw from it in the stories she crafts in her lyrics, musical arrangements, and the catchy, sometimes hypnotic melodies of her songs.<br /><br />In interviews, Haley's struggled to define the sound of her album, but usually has settled on saying she was going \\"retro with a modern twist\\". On Listen Up! she was very successful at striking this tricky balance, as the album does have a throwback sound, but is definitely not old-fashioned.<br /><br />Haley's incredible versatility as an artist is also on display, as she successfully infused Motown, disco, rock, reggae, pop, soul, hip-hop, R&B, funk and even some hints of her famed jazz vocal stylings. Of course, no element on the album is more prevalent than Haley's sultry, sexy vocal tones that turn up the heat on virtually every song.<br /><br />When discussing the album, Haley's said she really wanted to make it raw and organic, but, if I have one criticism of Listen Up! it would be that production got a bit heavy-handed at times. I did appreciate some of the production effects, just not the lazy or gratuitous ones. Would have liked Haley's voice left alone more, and adding electronic drum beats and synth strings to certain arrangements only served to detract from her authenticity and allure. My suspicion is the producers decided it would make the album sound more current, but I disagree with this thinking. Haley arranged a great blend of old and new without needing such artificial imposition.<br /><br />That said, from this music lover's perspective \\"Listen Up\\" is a joyous feast for the ears, and will send you on a fantastic ride that can at times blow your mind. How refreshing to have a full 14 track album without any useless filler (you have GOT to buy the deluxe version). I enjoyed listening to Listen Up from beginning to end without ever reaching for the skip track button. VERY well done, Haley! You are truly gifted.<br /><br />Anyway, without further ado, here is my song-by-song review.<br /><br />Oh My! - Expected to soon be released as the 2nd single off the album, \\"Oh My!\\" harkens back to Donna Summer's \\"Love To Love You Baby\\" in the sense that Haley spends much of the song having powerful orgasms. The Motown arrangement is contrasted with deep blaring horns, heavy production, and a ridiculous rap interlude by B.o.B that's obviously a sellout to Top 40 radio. While this tune is certainly catchy and fun, it's also a bit of a noisy mess for me.<br /><br />Free - The first single off the album, Free is about mercifully ending a sour relationship as nicely as possible, and showcases Haley's bluesy, sultry, jazzy tones with minimal production. I particularly love the crashing symbols as she hits her opening verse, and the hard driving drum beat accompanying her vampy stylings. Definitely one of my favorites.<br /><br />Liar - A rock/soul vibe with a good back beat and cool phrasing, Haley seems to be willing to tell herself anything she needs to in order to stay with Mr. Wrong.<br /><br />Wasted Tears - A catchy Motown track about recovering from heartbreak, this song hearkens back to early Supremes with lots of horns. Think \\"Where Did Our Love Go\\". A fun song that makes you wanna get up and dance.<br /><br />Undone - A piano ballad straight from the Adele school of heartache. While Haley delivers a rich, heartfelt vocal, the synth strings are sappy and the song gets too whiny and repetitive for my tastes. Not my cup of tea, but if you like melodramatic pop ballads you'll love Undone.<br /><br />Now That You're Here - A retro, 70's disco groove with a soulful vibe, this track gets you up and dancing from start to finish. The light, sexy, breezy flow makes it a song everyone will want to sing and clap along with. I'd say it's the catchiest song on the album, because I can't stop singing it. A love song that makes you want a partner to boogie with.<br /><br />Wonderland - A true retro, Motown throwback, one wonders who she's singing about being her man for the past year and a half? (cough...Stefano...cough) I like it, it's got a nice beat and is a sweet love song, but it gets a bit repetitive with the \\"Whoa, Whoa, Whoa, Wonderland\\" chorus.<br /><br />Keep Coming Back - A funky tune about an ex-lover who keeps working his way back again. Shows off Haley's more forceful vocals, and she tosses in a hint of growl for good measure, too! Good change of pace song, but would have liked it better less synthesized. Some cool guitar licks from Haley's father Harry.<br /><br />Hit The Ground Running - Damn, Haley can write some sexy lyrics. This one's about Haley seducing a guy and then dumping his ass when he falls too deep for her. A killer torch song aching for vamp Haley to get the guys hot and bothered with a blues-rock edge instead of the sunny Motown beat. Still sexy and sultry, but catch her live performances of this one. They're so hot.<br /><br />Walking On Heaven - Another classic 70's disco track, on this one Haley's seeing the world through happy, rose colored lenses and is ready to sing and dance about it. I'm only too happy to sing and dance along with her! Synth heavy, but since it's a disco track it seems appropriate.<br /><br />Follow Me - An interesting track. Heavy synthesized distortions are over-the-top, but the reggae is pretty awesome. Not sure how I feel about this one yet.<br /><br />Spiderweb - This haunting, psycho-sexual rock thriller is absolutely mind blowing. I found myself addicted from the first listen, and consider it the most powerful song on the album. Vocally, Haley's at her seductive, femme fatale best. Love the tender, lullaby piano opening as Spiderweb gradually builds to full on rock before ending with the lullaby piano again. Think \\"Gold Dust Woman\\" meets \\"Whole Lotta Love\\". If this song doesn't turn you on you must be dead.<br /><br />What You Don't Know - A sweet song about new love emerging from friendship, this classic 70's disco track pulls you onto the strobe-light dance floor rocking the polyester leisure suit. Dust off the disco ball, and shake your groove thing, baby!<br /><br />Let's Run Away - Haley shows off her Reggae chops, and guess what: She's absolutely awesome!! I love the way her tone meshes with the reggae beat, and the track's fun, loving vibe is infectious. Who knew? Get me a margarita on the beach and let me dance to Haley.<br /><br />Final rating: Haley Reinhart's \\"LISTEN UP!\\" (Deluxe) = 5/5 Starserself anything she needs to in order to stay with Mr. Wrong.<br /><br />Wasted Tears - A catchy Motown track about recovering from heartbreak, this song hearkens back to early Supremes with lots of horns. B007JRU2UK|2.0|The Descendants.  What does that say to you?  Somehow I missed everything about this movie, trailers and reviews, except for the part of it being shot in Hawaii.  I had no idea what to expect, although someone wrote it was like \\"every other George Clooney movie\\".  I can't say either way, even after seeing it.  To me, the title could be for a Sci-Fi Horror flick, where the Descendants are those of Martians who impregnated women five generations ago, and now, after passing from womb to female child from generation to generation, they're ready to pop out, after devouring the living woman in the most gory fashion, of course, and terrorize the audience for the next ninety minutes, and four sequels.  Or something.<br /><br />This movie is not that movie.  It could probably use a better title.  It opens with a panoramic shot of Diamond Head and Waikiki environs, shot from a tower or hillside house near Punchbowl, or thereabouts.  Then there's a shot of a nice blonde woman in a speeding boat.  One gets the impression this boat ride went bad, but one never sees it.  Instead, George Clooney's voice, sounding bitter, is talking about living in paradise and how everyone seems to think that makes life better.  Instead, he asks (and I paraphrase) \\"does that make our diseases less fatal?  Our families less screwed up?\\".  Yep, the whole film set up right there.  It turns out that the pretty lady in the boat is Clooney's wife, and she fell out of boat and is in a coma.  She's going to die, that's not the focus of the movie, as she left a living will, so we'll move along.  I can't tell you what happens, but I can say the marriage was not going well, George spent too much time in the office;  there are two daughters, one of which looks more Hawaiian than anybody else in the film, which begs the question, who was HER father?  The older daughter has a cave man for a boyfriend, or at least that's what you think until Grandpa, father of the dying woman, punches him.  Yep, you can't tell the kids from the adults these days.  So there are family issues.<br /><br />But, the Family, is also a trust, holding 25000 acres of pristine Kauai ocean front property, and the trust will expire in seven years.  Fear Not.  The movie does not cover even one year.  So, they're meeting to talk about selling the land.  The sale will make them all filthy rich, and some of them need the money.  At this point it's worth pointing out that the trust dates back over 100 years, to A Real Hawaiian Princess (we thought Hollywood was over this enchantment with native princesses, but apparently not).  She was a direct descendant of King Kamehameha, but she married a haole (a white dude, although the word, like Gringo, originally meant stranger, and was first used on the Chinese) and that's how we can have a movie set in Hawaii with almost no Hawaiians in it.  Or Filipinos, Chinese, etc etc.  (For those of you who are pineapple starved:  King Kam unified the islands under his rule in the 18th century.  The caboose end of that dynasty was King Kalakaua, who blew the wad on two world tours and a grand marriage based on that of Emperor Napoleon III and Empress Eugenie, who at least had an empire to pay for it).<br /><br />So, once we establish that A) Mom Is Going To DIe.  and that B) We Must Sell The Land (we think).  The only thing left to do is to find out what they do with the land, and how George will react to the news that his nest has been invaded by a real estate geek---I'm sure the actor is a real nice guy, but if you already have George Clooney, would you really be going after a geek?  I suppose so, but I don't like it.<br /><br />The older daughter and her boyfriend are developed nicely.  The little girl remains something of a pet human.  The cousins, aunts and uncles, are nothing but talking background, and Dad In Law is the Character.  George Clooney plays the sort of fatherly role once played by Fred MacMurray and Robert Young (if you don't know who they are, you ARE young, and aren't missing much, but you should ask around from time to time, in case you miss the start of the war) and while he's quite sympathetic and likeable, we never really get to know him.  No one ever seems to realize that it is HIS wife who is comatose, despite their failings.  I think the movie would have been better with a bit more of a focus on him, along with the daughters.<br /><br />In the end, given that the impetus of the film is set by the dying woman, once she's gone and all the questions are answered, about her and the land (it's on his side, not hers) you've pretty much had the experience.  Watch it again?  Maybe.  I'm not so sure.  I give stars as much for watchability in reruns, and three stars is a movie I'd buy, or already own.  I give this a two.  It's a run of the mill dying person story, sharing the plot with a family business drama, and minus the Love quotient that would have made the dying part sufficient to carry the film.  So, while it's worth a first try, I'd put it half way down your list with a view towards a hurricane evacuation;  a very rainy day, or when you're snowed in, next winter.  Set in a nice Sonoma red and some cheese and do it in style.

B000002KO3|3.0|Shortly after completing \\"Remain In Light\\", his attempt to fuse West African beats with Talking Heads music, Eno recorded a real Afro-Beat group called Edikanfo.  Quote from Eno in his biography, On Some Faraway Beach:  \\"The experience (recording Edikanfo) was 'depressing', he later admitted, simply because it made his own African-inspired with Talking Heads seem 'wooden by comparison'.  I wouldn't be surprised if David Byrne felt the same way.<br /><br />That's pretty much what you get with Remain In Light.  It sounds like Brian Eno the band enthusiastically attempting to make their own new wave version of Afro-Beat.<br /><br />The music sounds rushed, not in terms of hurried production, but in terms of the beat. Everything is slightly ahead, making the album FEEL nervous or high strung (or wooden, as Eno called it).  It doesn't really groove.  It sounds metronomic. In a way, that is what makes it distinctive and unusual.  It doesn't sound like anyone else at the time and didn't sound like Fela Kuti.  Listening out of context now, without influence of the press or the 'scene' in 1980, I can understand that it was really unusual then.  David Byrne's voice and phrasing really make it special.<br /><br />Since we live in an age where it's easy to make comparisons, seek out Edikanfo (or look at video clips online) and compare it to Remain In Light.  Robbing something of it's groove is an interesting effect, but is it really better?  Would the album have been better using real African musicians?  Who knows?

Here's an example from one page in \\"The Gentlemen's Hour\\":<br /><br />Boone goes to Starbucks ... and endures the skepticism that results from his order of \\"a medium black coffee.\\"<br /><br />\\"You want an Americano Grande?\\" the barista asks him.<br /><br />\\"A medium black coffee.\\"<br /><br />\\"Grande.\\"<br /><br />\\"Medium,\\" Boone says, gesturing to the cups. \\"The size between large and small.\\"<br /><br />\\"That's a grande.\\"<br /><br />\\"There we go.\\"<br /><br />\\"Your name?\\" the barista asks.<br /><br />\\"My name?\\"<br /><br />\\"So we can call you.\\"<br /><br />\\"For what?\\"<br /><br />\\"For when your Americano Grande is ready.\\"<br /><br />\\"I thought you'd just pour it.\\"<br /><br />\\"We have to make it,\\" the barista says. \\"Then we'll call you.\\"<br /><br />\\"Boone.\\"<br /><br />\\"Boo?\\"<br /><br />\\"Just use Daniels.\\"<br /><br />\\"Thank you, Daniel.\\"<br /><br />He stands there waiting for his coffee. She looks at him kind of funny, then points to her right and says, \\"It will be over there. They'll call you.\\"<br /><br />\\"Got it.\\"<br /><br />He slides over to his left and waits behind another couple of caffeine communicants who receive their cappuccinos and macchiatos with appropriate reverence, then hears, \\"Daniel.\\"<br /><br />\\"Thanks.\\"<br /><br />\\"You got it.\\"<br /><br />He takes his coffee into the main part of the place and sits down in an over-stuffed easy chair. About the only one there without a laptop computer, he feels like an old man going to the newspaper rack and taking a physical copy of the New York Times, printed on something called paper, and goes back to his chair. People like up, slightly annoyed, when the turns the page and makes a rustling noise.<br />  (end of book excerpt)<br /><br />In my opinion, this is brilliant writing. If you've enjoyed Winslow's other novels, or enjoy writers such as Elmore Leonard, Robert Crais, or John Sandford, don't miss this Winslow novel.ightly annoyed, when the turns the page and makes a rustling noise.<br />  (end of book excerpt)<br /><br />In my opinion, this is brilliant writing. B006588YUE|4.0|I don't have air conditioning in the summer, and I don't like the idea of water cooling, so I was concerned that I may be building a noisy air-cooled monster. I was resigned to live with a bit of noise, since I had another dead silent system which would be powered up most of the time.<br /><br />I figured that I would order the Intel stock air cooled thermal solution, BXRTSLGA2011AC, until I could find something better suited to my needs. Yes, I was aware that almost everyone criticises the stock Intel HSF. But for $19.99, I wasn't gonna keep about a grand worth of parts laying on the table waiting for assembly.<br /><br />When I got the Intel HSF, initial inspection left me with the feeling that this would be a VERY TEMPORARY stop gap solution.<br /><br />The Heatsink was of modest size, and the fan blew down upon it, Which I liked, but it was almost entirely constructed of aluminum. Only a thin sliver of copper was used on the bottom to make contact with the processor's heat spreader.<br /><br />What was even more shocking, and disappointing was that the heatsink's contact area was round, and only covered approximately 60 percent of the processor's heat spreader!<br /><br />I THOUGHT NO WAY WAS THIS THING GONNA DISSIPATE 130 WATTS OF GENERATED HEAT!<br /><br />The heatsink came with a thin layer of thermal paste pre-applied. With thirty years of dealing with computers, one realizes that manufacturers prepare their products for distribution assuming the worst situations in the field. They are much more concerned that something will at least work out of the box, rather than work well. Thus one finds heatsinks with too much thermal paste. This will assure the vendor that at least \\"some\\" sort of contact will be made, but not necessarily the best contact.<br /><br />I removed about 80 percent of the thermal compound that was pre-applied. I spread the remaining 20 percent into the thinest layer posssible on the heatsink contact area.<br /><br />Mounting the HSF went fairly well. I prefer the new spring loaded screws to the older push-pins and fancy latch contraptions. The screws had to be pushed down quite a way before they would catch. Attention also had to be paid to the order of tightening the screws.<br /><br />Intial power-up gave me one of the most pleasant surprises I have had in a long time.  The system was very quiet, almost silent. In fact, so quiet that if I wanted to tune for noise, I could probably make it silent.<br /><br />The temps were so low that I figured maybe I wasn't pushing it hard enough.<br /><br />The Heatsink was to cool an Intel XEON E5-1620,  very similar to i7-3820<br />So I ran 8 threads of Prime95 @ 3.6GHz<br />With an open case, and room temps of about 72F, the CPU never rose above 122F and the fan hovered around 1900 RPM!<br /><br />Bottom Line: If one knows how to apply it correctly, this Heatsink will cool a 130 watt TDP processor, running 8 threads, full tilt, very quietly. (Not quite silent at 1900 RPM but still very quiet).  Also, this is half the weight and size of full tower Heat sinks which can cause your board to flex and short out.

The author is English, and the characters are American, Washington residents, but you can swear they lived in Surrey, England for half their lives. How many American college students do you know \\"fetch\\" things, \\"ring\\" up their friends, and go on \\"holiday\\"? Oh let's not forget the 101 times the words \\"crap\\" was used by the main character. And if it wasn't just crap alone, it was \\"holy crap\\", \\"triple crap\\" or \\"super crap.\\" Has the author been to America or has she gotten her ideas while watching Twilight? And mix this in with language from the early 20th century, too formal to be spoken in 2012, and throw in some pretty hardcore out of place SAT words. My old high school English teacher, would have had his red marker out ready to make corrections with all the questionable syntax after reading this book. Was it proof read? And the amount of times the main characters are either muttering, murmuring, or whispering their words....Let's not even go there....<br /><br />Ok so the writing sucks, but how is the story? Let's get to the story and the characters....<br />So Christian Grey is this billionaire (not a millionaire, but a super billionaire), who is a fluent French speaker (which is quite believable), but he is also a classical pianist, a licensed helicopter pilot, athletic, gorgeous; a perfect Adonnis to be exact and the perfect lover. He is also a self made man, but he uses his money to combat world hunger. Oh yeah and all of this at the ripe old age of 27! Where did he have time to do all of this? And on top of that, he's never seen working. Instead, his time is spent having sex or thinking about sex (sex addict perhaps?) or texting, and emailing the female character, Anatasia. Expect Anatasia to have an email back from Christian instantly. How can the ultimate bachelor like Christian, the CEO, the extremely busy, powerful Adonnis that he is seem so available....Now let's get to the female character, Anna. Anna is a shy, naive, clumsy, inexperienced, pretty, but not drop dead gorgeous, intelligent, but not too sure of her self young woman, who just just graduated from college. But rather than this character being likable, she became annoying with her constant contemplating between her \\"subconscious\\" and her \\"inner goddess\\" and bickering. And then there's the repetition...expect Anatasia to bite her lip 101 times and her subconscious to say \\"crap\\" another 202 times. I will not get to the repetitious erotic words that were used that could of used a bit more imagination...Lets keep this PG rated.<br /><br />Then comes the sex scenes, but that's what the book is all about right? The juicy writing. The book started out a bit slow, and the the juicy stuff didn't take place until a quarter way through the book. The author really tried to build you up to what was coming up ahead. I would say the first steamy situation is the best, and I have to admit good and pretty entertaining, because it kept me interested to read more, but this is probably the best part of the book because after a couple more steamy encounters, it becomes too repetitious and too unbelievable that it becomes more laughable than erotic. It's a very long book. 500 pages to be exact, and if it was good, it wouldn't be a problem, but it loses its steam after 2/3 of the way through and it becomes too redundant and boring. By page 350, I was readyfor the story to end.<br /><br />So will I read the rest of the series? No. I think I'll save my $40. I guess this goes to show sex sells.

B000G8NXYQ|4.0|I love Federico Fellini.  I find his approach to film to be just beautifully composed; the perfect mixture of the serious and the lighthearted.  He understood how to combine these elements in a way that not only told a story but conveyed an emotion that was enriched by a feeling of true depth and authenticity.<br /><br />His films always felt grounded in reality.<br /><br />Of my favorite directors, he probably ranks second only to Jean-Luc Godard, who used that same balance to create films that were grounded in the complete opposite; the absurd.  Godard's films always leaned heavily on their absurdity, using it as a way to convey his messages, which Fellini leaned in the other direction, using the honesty in his scope to get his point across.  Both filmmakers were pioneers for the way we perceive film today and both deserve their respective places in that cinephile's list of influential filmmakers, and both topple nearly every director attempting cinema today.<br /><br />`Amarcord' is a perfect example of Fellini's ability to convey feeling.  Throughout the film's running length we are filled with the delightfulness that is found in Fellini's nostalgic trip down memory lane.  As he fills the frame, and our hearts, with these colorful creatures (not all as effective or enjoyable as the others) we cannot help but be intrigued by their presence and their inevitable effect on each other and ourselves.  Where `Amarcord' falls flat is in that development of story, which is something that isn't always necessary in film but something that felt particularly missing here.  I felt its lack thereof, and that to me means that it needed to be present.  The collection of random situations and events that link this seaside town together is all fun and games, but at the end of the day I feel as though I am left with little more than an emotion.  `Amarcord' doesn't affect my thinking or provoke my interest in anything substantial and it doesn't cause me to meditate on the events portrayed.<br /><br />It is sadly a tad forgettable.<br /><br />That isn't to say that the film is bad, for it is actually pretty great.  From a technical standpoint it is flawless.  The direction is sharp, the editing is key (it flows beautifully) and the cinematography is gorgeous.  The score is essential, for it draws out the desired emotions, and the acting as an ensemble is very good, with the exception of Armando Brancia, who is an annoyance and a bother.  Magali Noel is wonderful, and Bruno Zanin, who is probably the closest thing to a protagonist this film has, is also fantastic.<br /><br />I wish it had a linear storyline, only because I think it is missing that glue that would hold this collection of `scenes' together, but as a cinematic experience it is hard to fault Fellini's emotion filled ballad to his yesteryears.trayed.<br /><br />It is sadly a tad forgettable.<br /><br />That isn't to say that the film is bad, for it is actually pretty great. 0375704027|4.0|This is a book about death, about the idea of death. For this reason, I encourage everyone past coming of age to read it.  It will perhaps force you to contemplate the subject so often ignored. Norwegian Wood is a great place for such a start, with its honest, sobering meditation on the idea of death.<br /><br />The book is written in a vintage Murasaki style: slow, painfully at times, but beautifully prosed. Every page you get closer peak inside the headspace of a man, Watanabe, somewhat damaged by the death of his best friend. He is drawn to others who were just as damaged, perhaps seeking refuge in each other's communal defects. Unable to cope with reality, many characters throughout the book choose to end their lives, abruptly, as if to punctuate that their lives really meant nothing. Then the stories become about those whom they left behind, about their struggles and often failed attempts to cope. 'Death' doesn't end when people are buried; it continues on as scars, layering one after another, on their friends, families, and loved ones.<br /><br />Through all this, Watanabe learns to accept death as part of life. It's natural. It can't be fought. It can't be prepared for.  I love the ending of the novel: you get the feeling that he hadn't quite recover from the pain, and maybe never will, but at the same time, you feel hopeful for him.

B0079J27JW|5.0|These recordings are remakes of works that David Oistrakh recorded for EMI in the 1950's.  EMI had just signed Herbert von Karajan to a new contract after a nine year hiatus, and the opportunity to record him and his Berlin Philharmonic with the Soviet superstars Oistrakh, Richter, and Rostropovich in the Beethoven Triple Concerto was seen as a marketing coup.  The three Soviet artists were at the peak of their popularity in the 1960's.  The only downside is that the Beethoven Triple Concerto is not top-flight Beethoven. It is more entertaining than profound.  Fortunately, the artists  don't try to make more of the work than it can handle.  Everyone appears to be enjoying themselves, and EMI's recording, although a bit opaque and wooly, is perfectly acceptable.<br /><br />The Brahms pieces on this disc are sterner stuff.  Oistrakh had made the Double Concerto with Fournier and Galliera in the 1950's, a recording that still holds up very well.  This remake with Rostropovich and George Szell with the Cleveland Orchestra is as good as this elite grouping of artists would suggest.  This one of the best versions of the Brahms Double Concerto in the catalog.<br /><br />David Oistrakh was born to play the Brahms Violin Concerto.  Although displaying a prodigious technique, it was his ability to sustain a long lyrical line that set Oistrakh apart from other great violinists of his generation.  He plays with great lyrical intensity throughout the work, and he is superbly partnered by George Szell and his Cleveland Orchestra. This would be Oistrakh's third and final studio version of the work, and it would also be one of Szell's last recordings.  George Szell passed away in 1970, and David Oistrakh died in 1974.<br /><br />The sound on this recording is full, bright, with a warm orchestral perspective.<br /><br />The works on this recording are a stunning reminder of the great music that was being made in the Classical Golden Age.

Michael Stuhlbarg's Griffin character is one of the shining moments of the film. Griffin is able to see every future that could possibly happen (along with the past), but is never quite sure which future is actually going to occur until moments before it happens. His personality along with his enthusiasm has him steal the show at every turn.<br /><br />Rick Baker is the other highlight. He created so many aliens and creatures for this movie. Once the sequel gets going, it's visually very appealing and Rick Baker is one of the essential reasons for that. These aliens are just a wide variety of bizarre, vibrant, and hideously fascinating. Baker more than outdid himself.<br /><br />Despite a rough and rocky start, \\"Men In Black 3\\" is still decent. It's mostly due to the likes of Josh Brolin and Michael Stuhlbarg's fantastic performances along with Rick Baker's exceptionally creative creature designs, but the ending packs an emotional punch. \\"Men in Black 3\\" feels like the type of sequel that isn't completely necessary and doesn't really offer anything new, but is still a nice addition for fans of a beloved franchise.ky start, \\"Men In Black 3\\" is still decent. B007O4XX6S|3.0|I was looking forward to this album for a number of reasons; Jeff Lynne's production, Ringo Starr, and I've been waiting for a new Joe Walsh solo record for 20 years... but, this album was a disappointing in a number of ways.<br /><br />There are some great songs on here. Lucky That Way, The Band Played On and Spanish Dancer are among my personal favorites (your results may vary), but this record sounds so bad it could've been recorded by Eddie Kramer. It's plagued with overload distortion and really bad mixes. In fact, the opening guitars of Analog Man are clipping, and ironically, not in a good analog way. It sounds like Pro Tools ran out of headroom and we're hearing complete digital distortion. India is another song that is covered in overload distortion.<br /><br />And cranky audiophiles who don't like compression should stay away. I love the sound of compression, especially in rock music, but there comes a point where it makes things sound smaller, and I think they've hit the saturation point on this album. It makes the album sound pretty lifeless and listening to it wears you out.<br /><br />Then there's the inconstant tonality of the songs. The job of a mastering engineer is to make the album sound like a unified collection of music, but each song here sounds radically different. Analog Man is so mushy and lacking in clarity, while Lucky That Way is so hyped in the top and bottom end that it has has a distinct lack of midrange fullness, and that's followed by Spanish Dancer, a song recorded and mixed by Jeff Lynne, so the defining sound is the midrange heavy mix. Funny enough, there are two mastering engineers credited for this lack of consistency. Too many cooks, perhaps? I haven't heard such ineffective mastering since listening to a Steve Hoffman flat transfer.<br /><br />As for the music, Joe sounds as good as ever. There *are* great songs here (some were mentioned earlier in my review), and as can be expected, there are some real stinkers like the lame-o drum machine driven Funk 50. I doubt any of these songs will become Walsh classics like Rocky Mountain Way or Life's Been Good, but this collection of music is certainly better than his last couple of albums.<br /><br />And finally, I bought this on vinyl. It came with a hi-res 24/96 digital download which I thought was very cool. I hope this is a new trend with vinyl. I'm tired of getting useless 320kbps MP3 downloads. There's no reason all downloads can't be at least 16 bit nowadays. Anyway, getting back to the vinyl, both sides of my LP were pressed off center and it had more surface noise than I'd expect from a new record. Even after I cleaned it on my VPI 16.5 there was still a constant background of Rice Krispies(tm). This poor quality makes me assume it was pressed at United Record Pressing in Nashville.<br /><br />Even though I'm slightly underwhelmed, it's great that Joe Walsh finally released a new solo album. Nevertheless, I'm going to put Analog Man on the shelf and play Barnstorm a couple of times.

B0025X4PL0|5.0|FRAMES is simply phenomenal. I revisited this masterpiece today for the first time in awhile, and by a couple minutes into track one, decided I needed to add my glowing praises to its page on Amazon. I'm appalled to see only five others have commented so far, and can only hope this is in part because of the long (~18 month) and unfortunate delay between the original Fall 2007 release of FRAMES and its stateside availability.  To cut to the chase, FRAMES represents the peak of Oceansize's stunningly dense and brilliant career that spanned the years 2002-2010. Their previous two LPs were also undeniably five-star efforts, but this one is the most consistent and hard-hitting of them all. Those earlier records each contained multiple tracks which might contend for the title of all-time best OC song, yet they also faltered -- if only briefly -- with material just a peg or two short of the perfection they're capable of in their best form. FRAMES, on the other hand, doesn't miss a beat.  This is one of those rare discs I found myself jamming exclusively not for a mere week or two after release, but rather for MONTHS. My choice of favorite song cycled through all seven non-instrumentals, each time accompanied by the realization that I'd missed the best nuances on a track I'd previously found a tad lacking. It's also the most cohesive Oceansize record, rewarding an uninterrupted front-to-back listen every bit as much as DARK SIDE OF THE MOON. The way it communicates such a specific, palpable mood and atmosphere, while remaining dynamic enough to weave in both &#34;Savant&#34; and &#34;Sleeping Dogs,&#34; is nearly unparalleled in modern rock. As an avid fan of rock music spanning various sub-genres, I don't take saying this lightly: FRAMES is perhaps my favorite album of all time, and certainly in the top 5.  4.9 stars

This book weaves through their different lives and expresses the difficulties they face, not only in understanding each other, but understanding themselves as well as they struggle to be their own people, despite the expectations of others.  In a sense it also includes the grandmothers, because as the mothers tell their stories, of growing up in China and leaving, their mothers play a big role in shaping their lives and decisions.  It also shows their decisions with where to take their lives and the reactions when something happens that wasn't planned for.  A noticeable story is where the Joy Luck Club name came from and how the actual club was originated, told by the mother of Jing-Mei Woo.<br /><br />I have to say I enjoyed the mothers' stories more than the daughters'.  They just seemed to have more depth to them.  Their struggles seemed more poignant while the daughters' lives dealt more with divorce and jobs. I found it too formulaic and predictable.<br /><br />The story of the slave condition has been told repeatedly through the years, and Grissom doesn't much change the route.  It's an important subject for discussion as long as the elements provide new insight, but there's not much that's fresh here.  There's the faint and delicate white wife who suffers through too many tragedies to see the world clearly.  There's an absentee plantation owner who puts aside family and farm in a distant quest for a hazy goal.  That, of course, leaves the plantation and its slaves in open turmoil because of a sadistic overseer and randy male laborers who intimidate and impregnate the helpless females.  Of course the owner has his own way with the black women while his wife turns a blind eye to the results.<br /><br />There are beatings, incestuous encounters, murders, and forced starvation, all elements found in abundance in the history of slavery.  There are also, in Grissom's book, kindly elders who try to keep relationships cordial and familial ties bound together, but find good intentions are unable to cope with the reality of a savage atmosphere.  The author does not produce lurid or overly graphic details in her writing, a welcome change to some earlier renditions of the slavery story, but that makes the overall atmosphere, while threatening, somewhat tepid.<br /><br />I finished the book because the author was able to keep me interested in her well-defined cast of characters.  The concept of a white indentured servant coexisting with black slaves, and being accepted into both the white and black family structure, is a new twist on an old story, one that I wanted to follow.  Grissom did a nice job of keeping a complex character structure under control and comprehensible.<br /><br />Most of the readers in my book club thought highly of THE KITCHEN HOUSE and I don't disagree.  The quality of the writing is excellent and the scenes are well constructed.  If it weren't for a rather familiar story I would have rated it a little higher.  But I certainly encourage the reading of the book.  I might be getting too picky in my old age.  You be the judge.<br /><br />Schuyler T Wallace<br />Author of TIN LIZARD TALES

B007HO392S|4.0|Carrie Underwood releases her fourth studio effort on the support of first single \\"Good Girl,\\" which much like [[ASIN:B002DYJAIY Play On]] lead single balances country and pop. Similarly, [[ASIN:B007HO392S Blown Away]] balances country and pop among other genre influences throughout it duration.  For the most part, Blown Away is a sound effort with abundant enjoyable moments.  There are cuts that are flawed and lack the focus and precision of the best cuts, much like many albums these days. A testament to the album itself is the fact that first single \\"Good Girl\\" is not its very strongest cut - there are other standouts alongside that one.<br /><br />\\"Good Girl\\" opens the effort with a nice driving groove and fierce sounding electric guitars.  Carrie sounds incredibly commanding, aided by clear vocal production.  The song is `country' by name mostly, as there is inherent pop sensibilities written all over this cut.  If \\"Good Girl\\" does anything for Underwood, it certainly sheds her `American Idol' image and finds her a more polished performer.  \\"Blow Away,\\" the infamous title track that Underwood performed on Season 11 of American Idol, is less distinct and exciting than \\"Good Girl.\\" Better than her bland take of the song on the broadcast, \\"Blown Away\\" is by no means sub-par, but is lacking in character and development of the very best cuts.  On a positive note, the `four-on-the-floor' pounding beat is desirable as well as vocal production and overall production.<br /><br />The album's best track come through \\"Two Black Cadillacs,\\" a cut that opens with mysterious production and a somewhat understated rhythmic groove. The songwriting and Carrie's vocal performance are amongst their best here.  The refrain screams `country music' all over with its narrative lyrical approach: \\"...and the preacher said he was a good man/and his brother said he was a good friend/but the woman in the two black veils didn't bother to cry...\\"  Add to the clever use of background vocals, and the strength of the cut is further accentuated.  \\"See You Again\\" has a hard act to follow, but does sound job by contrasting the country-laden \\"Two Black Cadillacs\\" in favor of a more crossover-leaning cut.  The refrain is epic and big in sound and the production top-rate intact with pedal steel, guitars, and piano.  The tender bridge contrasts the somewhat predictable verses adding some variety to the cut.<br /><br />\\"Do You Think About Me\\" stands out more than \\"See You Again,\\" contributing a folksy-country sound.  The four-on-the-floor thud definitely adds a `plus' to this cut, as does Underwood's strong vocal prowess on the refrain. If a quibble was to be issued, it would be \\"Do You Think About Me\\" is predictable.  \\"Forever Changed\\" is yet another contrast, finding Underwood aiming for more `tender' approach.  The results are a sound that is quite understated and lovely, though not particularly exciting.  \\"Nobody Ever Told You\\" accelerates the tempo predictably, but does feature some excellent musical touches.  The main rub with \\"Nobody...\\" would be that the music drags on a bit too long.<br /><br />\\"One Way Ticket\\" has a lazy feel about the groove, not to mention the use of whistles throughout, which makes it sort of captivating.  Overall well produced, Underwood shines, particularly on the gargantuan refrain.  A bit gimmicky for the country singer, \\"One Way Ticket\\" is easily more successful than not ultimately.  \\"Thank God For Hometowns,\\" a `B' cut unsurprisingly slackens the tempo.  It opens initially without percussive accompaniment with a groove finally entering in subtly; by the second iteration of the chorus the cut becomes more involved musically. \\"Good in Goodbye\\" is solid, though not the most exciting showing while \\"Leave Love Alone\\" feels as if it lacks `substance.' That is not to say it is `bad' or `inappropriate,' but \\"Leave Love Alone\\" feels as if it needs more lyrics or quality songwriting for whatever reason.<br /><br />After a slight `fall-off,' the superb \\"Cupid's Got A Shotgun\\" resurrects any conceded momentum.  The uptempo cut instantly captures one attention with its overt country sound, which fits Underwood perfectly.  The instrumental jam session only adds to the authenticity placing this cut in the upper echelon.  \\"Wine After Whiskey\\" is none too shabby as a penultimate cut, though not the `best of the best.'  \\"Who Are You\\" features a casual, driving country sound to close out the effort.<br /><br />Overall,  [[ASIN:B007HO392S Blown Away]] attempts to please both the country and pop worlds.  Aside from \\"Good Girl,\\" some of the best moments of Blown Away are the more overt country turns.  \\"Two Black Cadillacs\\" and \\"Cupid's Got A Shotgun\\" are easily two of Underwood's best songs she's released and both are non-concessions to pop or country-pop.  For the most part, Blown Away should please audiences, even when it doesn't `blow you away.'' the superb \\"Cupid's Got A Shotgun\\" resurrects any conceded momentum. What a ride!<br /><br />In many ways, I like John Puller better than Jack Reacher.  John is a Warrant Officer: the only Army investigating personnel to be sent on this mission. He's not a loner in real life: he has family ties and stays in touch....an older brother, apparently a nuclear engineer, imprisoned for life for treason...a father with Alzheimer's who served the Army well but never managed to get those stars.  John travels with luggage; he loves cats and takes very good care of his cat AWOL; he drinks Deer Park water and lots of coffee; and he plays well with others and seems to like the ladies.  Besides Sam, the town policewoman...there's a friendly one star general who knew a certain highly classified officer had found \\"something\\" in Drake before he was murdered.<br /><br />Baldacci uses the word \\"gravitas\\" a good bit when describing a few Army personnel Puller encounters on his mission.  Even though I couldn't find the word in my Webster's New Collegiate.....I believe it applies to Puller also.  When he discovers the problem in Drake, West Virginia, he knows how to throw his weight around for backup in a dangerous situation.  The problem, as I had previously thought, is not illegal strip mining! It's a far more serious and potentially dangerous threat to the residents of Drake...some six thousand \\"proud Americans with a little red around the neck.\\"<br /><br />The technology is very accessible...guns and other military \\"stuff.\\"  And Baldacci manages to reach a high tension level in several sections. At 434 pages, this thriller is a bit short for Baldacci, and I have two small complaints that shave that initial five star rating a bit.  One, there are apparently no plans for the author to write another Puller novel...and two, the ending got a little messy.  About twelve pages out, the novel could have ended sadly but with a good resolution.  Baldacci kept tying up loose ends that were not loose!<br /><br />So I guess I'll look forward to another \\"Jack Reacher to the rescue\\" and keep an eye on the best-seller list for another Puller!<br /><br />Jane<br /><br />Comments welcome.before he was murdered.<br /><br />Baldacci uses the word \\"gravitas\\" a good bit when describing a few Army personnel Puller encounters on his mission. 0385344228|5.0|WOW is the first word that comes to mind after finishing this book. Defending Jacob is definitely NOT what I expected it to be. I was anticipating a somewhat inspirational story of the justice system gone-wrong. The book opens with a teenage boy who accused of murdering a classmate. His father (an Assistant District Attorney, ADA) switches roles to act as a defense counsel for his 'railroaded' and 'falsely accused' son.<br /><br />The book is written from the perspective of Jacob's father Andy Barber (the former ADA) with the storyline and perspective rotating between the past and the present. It's actually somewhat confusing at first. Confusing in the sense that you aren't quite sure what is going on and where the writer is going with that particular set of information. In present form, Andy will reflect on things he should have paid more attention to, and how he wished he could have stopped and derailed what happened in the end. Then the story will switch gears and return to Jacob's murder trial. So, as the reader, you become acutely aware very early on that something bad is on the horizon. Obviously, the assumption is that Jacob will be found guilty in the end. I don't want to include too many spoilers, but there is much more to the story than guilty or not guilty. Frankly, the story bares a candid resemblance to the Casey Anthony saga. Jacob's parents are caught in a media storm and are faced with countless lies and excuses from their only son. Who can they trust? Who are they to believe? Although it is their son who is on trial, they find that they themselves are also on trial. Are they really helping their son by defending him? In the end, everything is lost. Their savings, their reputation in the community, and their prized family, all snatched away in the blink of an eye.<br /><br />The main thing that really stood out to me in this novel was that it offered no ultimate closure. I was left with the impression that either Jacob was one of the most unluckiest teenagers in the world or that he was a wolf in sheep's clothing -- a calculated killer. I found the lack of closure to be a true reflection of many of the cases we see in US courts today. Many families and government officials walk away from cases without the definitive answers they seek. So in that sense, the ending was logical and true the world we live in. On the other hand, I found the end extremely frustrating and undoubtably sad.<br /><br />Defending Jacob is compelling, suspenseful, and heartbreaking ... Undoubtably one of my favorite reads this year.teenagers in the world or that he was a wolf in sheep's clothing -- a calculated killer.
